<div id="review_178303753" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/178303753" />
    <a title="Vegantrav" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/834122-vegantrav"><img alt="Vegantrav" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1457275293p2/834122.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/178303753?book_show_action=true">Jun 20, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Vegantrav" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Vegantrav" href="/user/show/834122-vegantrav">Vegantrav</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781847679383" href="/book/show/9536930-incognito">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer178303753" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13175166929885086931">Do you believe in libertarian free will or Cartesian dualism? If so, David Eagleman’s <i>Incognito</i> will radically challenge your beliefs.<br/><br/><i>Incognito</i> is probably the best work of nonfiction that I have read this year (2011), and it is also one of the best books on neuroscience that I have read in quite some time. Some of the material here has been presented elsewhere (if you have read works on neuroscience or consciousness by scientists and philosophers like Antonio Damasio, V. S. Ramachandran, Joseph</span>
  <span id="freeText13175166929885086931" style="display:none">Do you believe in libertarian free will or Cartesian dualism? If so, David Eagleman’s <i>Incognito</i> will radically challenge your beliefs.<br /><br /><i>Incognito</i> is probably the best work of nonfiction that I have read this year (2011), and it is also one of the best books on neuroscience that I have read in quite some time. Some of the material here has been presented elsewhere (if you have read works on neuroscience or consciousness by scientists and philosophers like Antonio Damasio, V. S. Ramachandran, Joseph Ledoux, Alva Noe, Patricia Churchland, and Daniel Dennett, much of the material in <i>Incognito</i> will be familiar to you), but Eagleman does an amazing job of showing how processes below the level of conscious awareness control much of our behavior and actually make us who we are.<br /><br />One of the most frightening yet enlightening case studies that Eagleman discusses in this book is that of a man who had been married for twenty years and lived a normal, law-abiding life when, suddenly and unexpectedly, this man developed an intense interest in child pornography and even attempted to solicit sex from a very young prostitute: this middle-aged heterosexual man suddenly found that he was a pedophile (although he never actually raped a child). At the same time as his sexual appetites were changing, he also began having very bad headaches, so his wife took him to see a physician, and eventually he was diagnosed with a brain tumor. When the tumor was removed, both his headaches and his problem with pedophilia disappeared. About a year later, however, the headaches returned, and so did his sexual interest in children. He returned to the neurologist, and it was discovered that his original tumor had not been completely removed and had started growing again, and upon cutting out the tumor a second time, he again lost his sexual interest in children.<br /><br />Another example of a tumor causing a sudden change in behavior that Eagleman discusses is that of, Charles Whitman, who in 1966 killed 16 people (and wounded 32 others) on the campus of the University of Texas. Prior to the shooting, Whitman’s behavior had begun to change dramatically, and he felt that something was wrong with him. In the suicide note he wrote before committing his mass murder, he asked that he be autopsied when he died. When an autopsy was carried out, a tumor was found to have been growing and pressing against his amygdala; the amygdala plays a key role in regulating aggression, fear, and social behavior. Whitman’s friends testified to the fact that, in the months leading up to the shooting, he had not really been himself. Whitman himself wrote, in his suicide note, that he no longer felt like himself and that he was struggling with violent urges that he could no longer control. Had he not had this tumor, it is almost certain that Whitman would not have become a killer.<br /><br />Eagleman presents numerous other examples which show that our behavior and personalities are determined to a much greater extent by physiological and chemical processes than the ordinary layperson might think. However, Eagleman also pays due respect to the effects of the environment in which we live as a factor in shaping our very selves, but as he points out, we can no more control the environment in which we find ourselves than we can control the physiological and chemical processes that cause our brains to grow and change.<br /><br />This book is not meant to be an argument against free will. It is not a philosophical treatise. What it is is a fascinating synthesis of the biological basis of the self and of consicousness. I highly, highly recommend it.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13175166929885086931" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1091965956" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1091965956" />
    <a title="Kalin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/741149-kalin"><img alt="Kalin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1502630193p2/741149.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1091965956?book_show_action=true">Oct 29, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kalin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kalin" href="/user/show/741149-kalin">Kalin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="ebook 9780307379788" href="/book/show/10999812-incognito">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/741149-kalin?shelf=reviewed-in-english">reviewed-in-english</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1091965956" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14031958322744774452"><i>29 Оct 2014:</i> Just finished editing the Bulgarian translation. My inner selves--as is their wont at the end of a road--are still in a jumble. A more coherent review coming soon. ;)<br/><br/>What I'd like to note right now is: this is another book I highly recommend to scientists and laymen alike. If you've ever struggled with questions such as "Telepathy? What do you mean, reading my mind? Am I supposed to have only <i>one</i> of them?" or "So who is the <i>real</i> me? The one who passionately believes in ahimsa and no</span>
  <span id="freeText14031958322744774452" style="display:none"><i>29 Оct 2014:</i> Just finished editing the Bulgarian translation. My inner selves--as is their wont at the end of a road--are still in a jumble. A more coherent review coming soon. ;)<br /><br />What I'd like to note right now is: this is another book I highly recommend to scientists and laymen alike. If you've ever struggled with questions such as "Telepathy? What do you mean, reading my mind? Am I supposed to have only <i>one</i> of them?" or "So who is the <i>real</i> me? The one who passionately believes in ahimsa and non-violence? Or the one who wants to beat all of you senseless, for being such a stupid, insensible bunch? Or the one who laughs on the sideline, high above and beyond?" (or with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/quotes/3203487" rel="nofollow noopener">Occam's <i>effing</i> razor</a>), <i>Incognito</i> has some answers for you.<br /><br />Sorry ... I meant suggestions. ;)<br /><br />~<br /><br /><i>20 Dec 2014:</i> This hardly counts as coherent, but ... here goes:<br /><br />Let me first say that I've appreaciated this book and the new insights (or confirmations of old ones) it gave me <i>a lot</i>. This is the only reason why I let myself spend so much time examining the ideas that didn't quite convince me. (Because, after decades of struggling against myself, I remain a perfectionist. Yes, yes, you already got the point about the sel<i>ves</i>, yes? :) If I didn't care enough for it, I wouldn't bother.<br /><br /><i>So, the ideas that didn't quite convince me:</i><br /><br />There are some iffy, oversimplifying assumptions here (just like in any popular science book). For instance, the experiment that shows that men find women with dilated pupils more attractive. Here's how Eagleman explains it:<br /><br /><blockquote>In the largely inaccessible workings of the brain, <i>something</i> knew that a woman’s dilated eyes correlates with sexual excitement and readiness. Their brains knew this, but the men in the study didn’t – at least not explicitly. The men may also not have known that their notions of beauty and feelings of attraction are deeply hardwired, steered in the right direction by programs carved by millions of years of natural selection. When the men were choosing the most attractive women, they didn’t know that the choice was not theirs, <i>really</i>, but instead the choice of successful programs that had been burned deep into the brain’s circuitry over the course of hundreds of thousands of generations.</blockquote><br /><br />However, do dilated pupils imply only <i>sexual</i> excitement? What if they demonstrate <i>any</i> sort of excitement: artistic, spiritual, the "wow, I just had a glimpse into the meaning of life" kind? I'm asking this from personal experience: I've noticed that pupils dilate when people switch into their deeper, more intense modes--whatever the reason. And, boy, don't I <i>love</i> them when they're like that! :D<br /><br />So, should we say that we're genetically programmed to feel attracted by people in their more intense modes? Is this only about procreation? Is it about creation? What else?<br /><br />(This is related to the question why--and if--we're attracted by people with larger eyes. Hello, South Korea. ;)<br /><br />Or listen to this one:<br /><br />To justify his claim that our brains do most of the work without the involvement (or indeed awareness) of our conscious mind, Eagleman supplies the following examples:<br /><br /><blockquote>In 1862, the Scottish mathematician James Clerk Maxwell developed a set of fundamental equations that uniﬁed electricity and magnetism. On his deathbed, he coughed up a strange sort of confession, declaring that „something within him“ discovered the famous equations, not he. He admitted he had no idea how ideas actually came to him – they simply came to him. William Blake related a similar experience, reporting of his long narrative poem <i>Milton</i>: „I have written this poem from immediate dictation twelve or sometimes twenty lines at a time without premeditation and even against my will.“ Johann Wolfgang von Goethe claimed to have written his novella <i>The Sorrows of Young Werther</i> with practically no conscious input, as though he were holding a pen that moved on its own.</blockquote><br /><br />I have nothing against the claim. I wonder, however, about the relevance of the examples. Of course, writing a <i>scientific</i> book, Eagleman can't quite say, "You know, maybe they did get their input from the outside." Modern science--at least the subset Eagleman adheres to--hasn't proven that human beings are capable of getting input from the outside. Therefore, it <i>must</i> be the result of the subconscious work of our brains. QED.<br /><br />(What really riled me here was ignoring Blake's phrase "against my will." I know people who write like that: under dictation, aye. Dwelling on topics and expressing voices that have nothing to do with their lives or dispositions. Wondering who these voices are. And since I'm not writing a <i>scientific</i> book but a personal review on Goodreads, I don't mind saying it. Feel free to explain it away. ;)<br /><br />Aaaand ...<br /><br /><b>The paradox of the paradox of free will<br /><i>The most baffling mystery of scientific thinking nowadays</i></b><br /><br />I. Just. Don't. Get it.<br /><br />So, there's a subconscious impulse to move my arm several hundred milliseconds before I grow aware of my intention to do so, let alone do it. So, maybe I--the conscious self--am not in charge of moving my arm. So, maybe there's no free will. It's already been decided, all of it. By something else.<br /><br />But...butbutbut, dear Mr Eagleman:<br /><br />What made you write <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7013269.Sum_Forty_Tales_from_the_Afterlives" title="Sum Forty Tales from the Afterlives by David Eagleman" rel="nofollow noopener">this book</a>? Rather than <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18159958.The_Last_Unicorn" title="The Last Unicorn by Peter S. Beagle" rel="nofollow noopener">that one</a>? How many milliseconds do you estimate it took your whatever-it-was-but-definitely-not-free-will to come up with the idea and find the proper words and put them in the proper places?<br /><br />What makes me write this comment? Rather than polish the Bulgarian translation for the last time? How many milliseconds here? Anyone?<br /><br />Can anyone please explain what I'm missing?<br /><br />...<br /><br />I guess the real problem here is how Eagleman (or <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7159951.13_Things_That_Don_t_Make_Sense" title="13 Things That Don't Make Sense by Michael Brooks" rel="nofollow noopener">Michael Brooks</a>, or-- the list goes on and on) defines "free will."<br /><br />I think this is the closest to a definition that Eagleman provides:<br /><br /><blockquote>Even in the face of all the machinery that constitutes you, is there some small internal voice that is independent of the biology, that directs decisions, that incessantly whispers the right thing to do? Isn’t this what we call free will?</blockquote><br /><br />Now, if his point is that free will is NOT independent of biology, that is, it takes into account our physical bodies and is influenced by them, I have no bones to pick. I'm yet to see something--<i>anything</i>--that's truly independent of anything else. (I am a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Holism" rel="nofollow noopener">holist</a>.)<br /><br />The bone-picking starts when I try to quantify this dependence. How much of what we do is determined by the "machinery"? How much, by something else? A lot of recent arguments I've seen leave me with the impression it's <i>all</i> "machinery." Which is as funny as any absolute claim. (This one included, yes. Didn't you just laugh a bit? ;)<br /><br />To be fair to Eagleman, he does go on to quantify some of the unconscious influences, and ultimately says that for his purposes, the question of free will is <i>irrelevant</i>. Free will may be there, or not--it doesn't change anything about his suggestions on improving the legal system by incorporating our understanding that many actions are outside our conscious control. Which is another idea that I love. I just wonder why he, too, had to go into this "free will" morass.<br /><br />Incidentally, the best approach I've seen to this issue--"best" as in most convincing and productive--was in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2031004.The_Broken_God__A_Requiem_for_Homo_Sapiens___1_" title="The Broken God (A Requiem for Homo Sapiens, #1) by David Zindell" rel="nofollow noopener">The Broken God</a>, where the protagonist was learning to discover his hidden biological programs and re-program them. However, I don't know what sources inspired Zindell for that.<br /><br />Interesingly, Eagleman seems to suggest something similar. He calls it <b>prefrontal workout</b>:<br /><br /><blockquote>So our new rehabilitative strategy is to give the frontal lobes practice in squelching the short-term circuits. My colleagues Stephen LaConte and Pearl Chiu have begun leveraging real-time feedback in brain imaging to allow this to happen. Imagine that you’d like to get better at resisting chocolate cake. In this experiment, you look at pictures of chocolate cake during brain scanning – and the experimenters determine the regions of your brain involved in the craving. Then the activity in those networks is represented by a vertical bar on a computer screen. Your job is to make the bar go down. The bar acts as a thermometer for your craving: If your craving networks are revving high, the bar is high; if you’re suppressing your craving, the bar is low. You stare at the bar and try to make it go down. Perhaps you have insight into what you’re doing to resist the cake; perhaps it is inaccessible. In any case, you try out different mental avenues until the bar begins to slowly sink. When it goes down, it means you’ve successfully recruited frontal circuitry to squelch the activity in the networks involved in impulsive craving. The long term has won over the short. Still looking at pictures of chocolate cake, you practice making the bar go down over and over until you’ve strengthened those frontal circuits.</blockquote><br /><br />It's a beginning. ;)<br /><br /><i>And now to the really great reminders:</i><br /><br />The <b>illusion-of-truth effect</b>:<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/quotes/4189495">https://www.goodreads.com/quotes/4189495</a><br /><br />Even though I'm very well aware of this beast, it's unbelievable how many times I've caught my opinions being swayed by it. :(<br /><br />A trick I love:<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/quotes/4189659">https://www.goodreads.com/quotes/4189659</a><br /><br />However, I usually do it by offering my friend two (or more) fingers to choose from. Well, finally we know how it works. ;)<br /><br />Finally, being a holist :P, I love the way Eagleman beats some sense into pure reductionists. Starting with his explanation of <b>emergence</b>:<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/quotes/4279987">https://www.goodreads.com/quotes/4279987</a><br /><br />He goes on with examples from our brains and general biology, and concludes that:<br /><br /><blockquote>A meaningful theory of human biology cannot be reduced to chemistry and physics, but instead must be understood in its own vocabulary of evolution, competition, reward, desire, reputation, avarice, friendship, trust, hunger, and so on.</blockquote><br /><br />Check out the other quotes I've liked too. Here be nuggets.<br /><br />Did I say the book is highly recommended? ;)<br /><br /><b>Further reading:</b><br /><br /><i>Your brain ain't asploded yet? Give it a shove!</i><br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/319014.The_Holographic_Universe" title="The Holographic Universe by Michael Talbot" rel="nofollow noopener">The Holographic Universe</a><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/12273377.Thinking__Fast_and_Slow" title="Thinking, Fast and Slow by Daniel Kahneman" rel="nofollow noopener">Thinking, Fast and Slow</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="14031958322744774452" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_158754918" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/158754918" />
    <a title="Jaylia3" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1794273-jaylia3"><img alt="Jaylia3" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1594391810p2/1794273.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/158754918?book_show_action=true">Apr 04, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jaylia3" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jaylia3" href="/user/show/1794273-jaylia3">Jaylia3</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer158754918" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5425426951457955537">This very interesting and thought provoking book by neuroscientist David Eagleman is a little disorienting. After all, based on the numerous observations and scientific experiments he details Eagleman’s conclusion is that we have no freewill. I may think I am considering options, making decisions, and choosing, for instance, what book to read, but according to scientists who study these things I am not in charge, if by “I” what I mean is the “I” that I know--my conscious mind. It’s not surprisin</span>
  <span id="freeText5425426951457955537" style="display:none">This very interesting and thought provoking book by neuroscientist David Eagleman is a little disorienting. After all, based on the numerous observations and scientific experiments he details Eagleman’s conclusion is that we have no freewill. I may think I am considering options, making decisions, and choosing, for instance, what book to read, but according to scientists who study these things I am not in charge, if by “I” what I mean is the “I” that I know--my conscious mind. It’s not surprising that drugs, alcohol, brain injury, and evolutionary forces exert power over us that we are not always aware of while it is going on, but according to the science Eagleman reports there is more to it than that. In an experiment in which people were asked to lift their fingers at the time of their choosing, the conscious brain impulse to move was preceded by unconscious brain activity. <br /><br />Is this proof that the conscious decision to move a finger is governed by the unconscious mind? I’m not sure. And if it is proof, would that carry over into every kind of decision? Does the unconscious mind really have invisible, almost god-like power over every thought and action?<br /><br />While I am not convinced that the freewill/determinism question has been fully answered--neuroscience is still a very young field of knowledge--the first five chapters of Incognito are full of fascinating, persuasive examples that demonstrate how the reality we perceive with our conscious minds bears sometimes only a rough resemblance to what is actually happening. When reading Incognito I frequently broke off to share these examples with whoever was around me. There are illustrations you can try yourself, for instance there is a graphic that allows you to prove to yourself that your eyes have a blind spot, a gap in vision that your unconscious brain fills in based on what is probably there. <br /><br />In the final chapters of Incognito Eagleman uses the latest information from brain science to draw logical but sometimes counterintuitive and unsettling conclusions about the future of the justice system. With little or no freewill, what should society do with criminals? Since the unconscious operates on a “team of rivals” model in which conflicting impulses struggle for control, Eagleman would have incarceration based on the neuroplasticity of the offender—that is on how likely it is that the criminal’s brain could respond to reconditioning techniques. Those who could be reconditioned so that they would no longer cause damage to society would be; those who couldn’t be reconditioned because of frontal lobe impairment or other brain defects would be warehoused. <br /><br />Even though neuroscience is still in its infancy there is a lot of riveting information here about how the brain works. You don’t have to agree with all the conclusions Eagleman draws in this book for it to be worth reading. Incognito is a great book for sparking deep and engaging discussions.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5425426951457955537" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_533347937" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/533347937" />
    <a title="Myth" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1355576-myth"><img alt="Myth" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1317531925p2/1355576.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/533347937?book_show_action=true">Feb 11, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Myth" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Myth" href="/user/show/1355576-myth">Myth</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="ebook 9780307379788" href="/book/show/10999812-incognito">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1355576-myth?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer533347937" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4186275093427856384">Disclaimer: I have not actually finished this book and do not know if I will.<br/><br/>As someone who's very interested in neurology this book does have it's good moments, but they're largely eclipsed by a bunch of dumbing down. <br/><br/>I don't blame Eagleman, I know it's people in the publishing industry who probably pushed this book to be like this. Following is my reaction to each element I found annoying. There's a summary at the end. <br/><br/>Dumbing it down: Too much repetition and unnecessary metaphors. I do not kn</span>
  <span id="freeText4186275093427856384" style="display:none">Disclaimer: I have not actually finished this book and do not know if I will.<br /><br />As someone who's very interested in neurology this book does have it's good moments, but they're largely eclipsed by a bunch of dumbing down. <br /><br />I don't blame Eagleman, I know it's people in the publishing industry who probably pushed this book to be like this. Following is my reaction to each element I found annoying. There's a summary at the end. <br /><br />Dumbing it down: Too much repetition and unnecessary metaphors. I do not know how many times I have to be told the same thing in different configurations in two to three pages. Okay, I get it. I took the information at face value the first time it was said. <br /><br />Generalizations: Eagleman makes a ton of sweeping generalizations. I know generalizations are easy, but they're inaccurate and more fit for a marketing campaign than a book. I know Eagleman knows this, because there are times when he takes the more cautious approach and then follows it up with more generalizations. Generalizations lead to assumptions that are not necessarily true, if at all. It's pop-science at it's "best." <br /><br />Language: The writing style in this book bounces around. There are sections I don't mind, but the use of "you" is incredibly obnoxious. Obviously Eagleman doesn't know the sort of audience he may have, who may think in this rare way, have this condition and so on. The usage of "you" is yet again something more suited to advertisements than books (unless it's self-help.) <br /><br />Examples/Excersizes: These are so annoying. For one, not all of them will work on an ebook and two, not all of them will work period. This is not a children's textbook, so I don't understand why there are these childish little games. A normal person can reason that the information is true without having the mind games they published in Highlights in the 90s. Not all them work either, for reasons that are covered in the book. <br /><br />For instance, finding your blind spot. I'm aware I have a blind spot, but the little test didn't work at all and for a simple reason, I know the object/dot is there. Everyone I tried the test on (mostly family members) couldn't get the spot to go away and turn into background fuzz either. Why exactly is it assumed that the mind would turn a spot into background fuzz anyway (even though you shouldn't be able to technically see either?) Why wouldn't some brains simply fill in the dot? <br /><br />Sources: I took both psychology and media studies in college. I was rarely impressed by my psychology classes. Few actually rely on current information or on experimentation. It's expensive to conduct experiment, but way more reliable. The people who really understand the way the typical person thinks are in marketing and media studies. They stay far more up-to-date and have the money and motivation to do studies. Eagleman relies heavily on psychology which bothers me. A lot of what is known in abnormal psychology is from neurology and from brain injury. Of course there are the therapies that are shown to work, but the ideas surrounding the <i>typical</i> person's psyche seem to be frequently bias and out-of-date.<br /><br />For instance, Eagleman write about what is basically subliminal messages. Images that are displayed too quickly for the conscious mind to process. There's a ton of research that shows that subliminal messages don't work and that message can't be processed without conscious recognition. This is why subliminal messages aren't used in media anymore and we should be skeptical of any study using such a method. We also have to note that there are layers to our consciousness and it's not as simple as Eagleman writes it to be. Again, I assume he knows this and has dumbed it down. <br /><br />Aside from the fact that subliminal messages have never been proven to work, it was found in studies that a message followed up too quickly by another message would push out the last one. Basically, make us forget what we just saw - in studies where they flash faces to figure out if someone is racist (or whatever) we probably are consciously processing what we're seeing, but we're not given much time to make a decision. In another example people picked out words that were flashed, we do process them, but if they happen too quickly it can make our memories more fuzzy and incoherent than normal so we'll remember random things. <br /><br />Studies like this are very prone to human error, bias and I don't have much faith in them. Soft sciences are squishy and should act sponge-like, but are too often isolated and cemented. People in the hard sciences understand the purposes of science and one of those is change. <br /><br />Additionally, the psychology reveals biases, sexist notions and homophobic culture, which is probably only normal for studies taken from 50 to 100 years ago. Does our species reproduce with the use of two specialized sexes? Yes, but does that mean we're wired entirely just for sex for the sake of sex. No, we're a social species and we rely on community. There are other mammal that get what they want without being the least bit polite to the other they are trying to fornicate with. A zoologist knows more about mammal behavior than an evolutionary psychologist. I wouldn't be surprised if hospital doctors, emergency room workers and proctologists know more about typical human behavior than a psychologist. <br /><br />Studies of other apes have shown sex to be a social behavior that strengthens community. There's also theories in other fields about why there'd be bisexuals (it seems specifically bisexual woman and maybe a sense of community among females to raise children.) In this book human sexuality and attraction is grossly oversimplified (at least as far as I've read.) If we were so hardwired to attractive and healthy people it shouldn't go against our ethical instincts to say "We should 'fix' ugly, sick people and only let beautiful healthy people reproduce." There's also some hypothesis about us being attracted to what we're most familiar with (which is usually our own appearance) so we'll look for people with appearances that remind us of ourselves (or perhaps our parents.) This would explain our notions about people being well matched or looking good together and beauty in the eye of the beholder. <br /><br />tl:dr - Book is dumbed down, uses too many generalizations and has some suspect and inaccurate information. <br /><br /> I don't know if I can finish this book. The information is not delivered in a fast-paced or intellectual way and, as such, is not really my style. I'll take my chances with Scientific American over this. </span>
  <a data-text-id="4186275093427856384" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <td colspan="2">
    <div class="updateActionLinks">
        <a class="right flag" rel="nofollow" id="flag_link533347937" title="Flag this review as inappropriate." href="/flagged/new?resource_id=533347937&amp;resource_type=Review&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F9827912-incognito">flag</a>





      <span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_review_533347937'><a id="like_count_review_533347937" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/533347937?resource_type=Review"><span class="likesCount">9 likes</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it gr-button gr-button--small" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F9827912-incognito">Like</a></span>
&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a href="/review/show/533347937?book_show_action=true&amp;from_review_page=1">see review</a>
    </div>

<div id="review_2477088980" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2477088980" />
    <a title="Farha Crystal" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/38087945-farha-crystal"><img alt="Farha Crystal" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1445535747p2/38087945.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2477088980?book_show_action=true">Jul 31, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Farha Crystal" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Farha Crystal" href="/user/show/38087945-farha-crystal">Farha Crystal</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38087945-farha-crystal?shelf=david-eagleman">david-eagleman</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2477088980" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7073472711458151948">Why can your foot move halfway to the brake pedal before you become consciously aware of danger ahead? Why do you hear your name being mentioned in a conversation that you didn’t think you were listening to? Why is it so difficult to keep a secret? How is it possible to get angry at yourself—who, exactly, is mad at whom? :D<br/><br/>Mr. Eagleman shows through examples how often our behavior is regulated by factors we don’t control. And, the answer to "Who/ What am I?" is a never-ending search process unti</span>
  <span id="freeText7073472711458151948" style="display:none">Why can your foot move halfway to the brake pedal before you become consciously aware of danger ahead? Why do you hear your name being mentioned in a conversation that you didn’t think you were listening to? Why is it so difficult to keep a secret? How is it possible to get angry at yourself—who, exactly, is mad at whom? :D<br /><br />Mr. Eagleman shows through examples how often our behavior is regulated by factors we don’t control. And, the answer to "Who/ What am I?" is a never-ending search process until the system burns out of itself. :D<br /><br />Our capacities for decision-making and the way of seeing the world are not the same among individuals because of our different brain circuitries. So when it comes to criminal behavior: can we be blamed for “choosing” to do something when we only “choose” to do so because we have a brain tumor?<br />Given the fixed state of the brain and the situation the criminal was in at the time of the crime - could he have done other than that which he did? But, won't that require at least one factor between his hardwired brain and nurture/training/experience to have been different? How much control the conscious mind has over its secret self? ... ... ...<br /><br />However, Mr. Eagleman made hasty generalizations on how criminals should be treated based on a couple of examples he mentions in the book which I don't like particularly!<br /><br />Favorite lines : <i>“Imagine for a moment that we are nothing but the product of billions of years of molecules coming together and ratcheting up through natural selection, that we are composed only of highways of fluids and chemicals sliding along roadways within billions of dancing cells, that trillions of synaptic conversations hum in parallel, that this vast egglike fabric of micron-thin circuitry runs algorithms undreamt of in modern science, and that these neural programs give rise to our decision making, loves, desires, fears, and aspirations. To me, that understanding would be a numinous experience, better than anything ever proposed in anyone’s holy text."</i><br /><br /><br />The book is definitely presented in a readable, easy to understand fashion but I didn't find a single new thing about the brain in this book ... well, that's not a brag/criticism. It's just the current conscious finding except I am dubious about my secret synaptic dance festival :P </span>
  <a data-text-id="7073472711458151948" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_558150421" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/558150421" />
    <a title="Book" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5686119-book"><img alt="Book" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1419967188p2/5686119.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/558150421?book_show_action=true">Mar 11, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Book" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Book" href="/user/show/5686119-book">Book</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781847679383" href="/book/show/9536930-incognito">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5686119-book?shelf=neuroscience">neuroscience</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer558150421" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1085239603042005359">Incognito: The Secret Lives of the Brain by David Eagleman <br/><br/>"Incognito" is a fascinating look into our brain and the secrets that it reveals. It's a wonderful book that covers recent findings of mainly the unconscious processes of our brains. Neuroscientist and best-selling author, David Eagleman takes the reader on a journey of discovery of our brains; an enjoyable and enlightening ride that makes the young field of neuroscience fun and informative. This instructive 304-page book is composed of </span>
  <span id="freeText1085239603042005359" style="display:none">Incognito: The Secret Lives of the Brain by David Eagleman <br /><br />"Incognito" is a fascinating look into our brain and the secrets that it reveals. It's a wonderful book that covers recent findings of mainly the unconscious processes of our brains. Neuroscientist and best-selling author, David Eagleman takes the reader on a journey of discovery of our brains; an enjoyable and enlightening ride that makes the young field of neuroscience fun and informative. This instructive 304-page book is composed of the following seven chapters: 1. There’s Someone In My Head, But It’s Not Me, 2. The Testimony of the Senses: What Is Experience Really Like?, 3. Mind: The Gap, 4. The Kinds of Thoughts That Are Thinkable, 5. The Brain Is a Team of Rivals, 6. Why Blameworthiness Is the Wrong Question, and 7. Life After the Monarchy.<br /><br />Positives: <br />1.	An engaging scientific book that is not only accessible but a treat to read.<br />2.	A well-researched book that is well referenced.<br />3.	A fascinating topic in the hands of a neuroscientist. At the end of the first chapter the author ask a long series of intriguing questions about the brain and proceeds to answer them throughout the book. Great stuff!<br />4.	The more we understand how the brain works, the better understanding we will have about ourselves and society. "The emerging understanding of the brain profoundly changes our view of ourselves, shifting us from an intuitive sense that we are at the center of the operations to a more sophisticated, illuminating, and wondrous view of the situation."<br />5.	The book is full of fun and illuminating anecdotes.<br />6.	The author has a wonderful science-writing style, he reveals a scientific discovery and proceeds to elaborate with interesting details. "The first thing we learn from studying our own circuitry is a simple lesson: most of what we do and think and feel is not under our conscious control."<br />7.	The history and evolution of this fascinating young field of brain science.<br />8.	How are vision really works. Dare I say it...it's eye-opening. "You’re not perceiving what’s out there. You’re perceiving whatever your brain tells you."<br />9.	The constructions of the brain. "Time" to know the truth.<br />10.	Amazing facts abound in this book. "Interestingly, schizophrenics can tickle themselves because of a problem with their timing that does not allow their motor actions and resulting sensations to be correctly sequenced."<br />11.	The chasm between what your brain knows and what your mind is capable of knowing. <br />12.	The roles that consciousness plays versus unconsciousness. "The irony is that a professional athlete’s goal is to not think. The goal is to invest thousands of hours of training so that in the heat of the battle the right maneuvers will come automatically, with no interference from consciousness."<br />13.	Evolution it does a species "good". "This underscores a simple but crucial point: the brain’s circuits are designed to generate behavior that is appropriate to our survival."<br />14.	In some respects this book elicits deep philosophical thoughts, "What you are able to experience is completely limited by your biology." Awesome examples provided!<br />15.	The prepackaged software of the brain. Instincts versus automatized behaviors. "Our innate behaviors represent ideas so useful that they became encoded into the tiny, cryptic language of DNA. This was accomplished by natural selection over millions of years: those who possessed instincts that favored survival and reproduction tended to multiply."<br />16.	Understanding why artificial intelligence as in robotics is so complex.<br />17.	How the "Team of Rivals" in the brain works. The rational system versus the emotional system. Neural battles. "Your behavior—what you do in the world—is simply the end result of the battles."<br />18.	Disorders of the brain. Fascinating disorders and the consequences. <br />19.	Do we have free will? What about a soul? Are other animals conscious? Big questions, captivating insights and the implications this may have for the law. "Change the brain, change the person".<br />20.	Find out the most dangerous set of genes. A forward-looking, brain-compatible legal system.<br />21.	The myth of human equality. An interesting idea that supports the author's contention that the law already recognizes that fact.<br />22.	A great final chapter that summarizes the finding of the book and the philosophical considerations. Nature versus nurture. "These examples demonstrate that it is neither biology alone nor environment alone that determines the final product of a personality. When it comes to the nature versus nurture question, the answer almost always includes both."<br />23.	 Links worked great and excellent bibliography.<br /><br />Negatives:<br />1.	How many times do I have to read about our friend Phineas Gage? This account is in practically every pop neuroscience book that I've read...and there have been many.<br />2.	The amount of revelations is directly correlated with how many books of this ilk you have read, that being said, this is one of the best books of this genre.<br />3.	There is so much that remains to be known. The books focus is on the unconscious at the expense of the conscious. <br />4.	The book lacks depth. Its target audience is the layperson.<br /><br />In summary, I really enjoyed this book. What a fun and revealing book this was. If I had to do it all over I would have pursued a career in neuroscience over engineering, it's that interesting to me. Eagleman does a wonderful job of interweaving the latest in neuroscience research with interesting stories. The case of mass murderer Charles Whitman as an example will give you pause. The book is provocative and everyone will take something of note from it. I will take the following, " What you are able to experience is completely limited by your biology." I highly recommend it!<br /><br />Further suggestions: "Subliminal" by Leonard Mlodinow, "How to Create a Mind" by Ray Kurzwell, "The Blank Slate" by Steven Pinker, "Are You Sure?" by Ginger Campbell, "The Myth of Free Will" by Cris Evatt, "Who's In Charge" by Michael S. Gazzaniga, "Free Will" by Sam Harris, "The Power of Habit" by Charles Duhigg, "The Ego Tunnel" by Thomas Metzinger, and "The Tell-Tale Brain" by V.S. Ramachandra. </span>
  <a data-text-id="1085239603042005359" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_485040454" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/485040454" />
    <a title="Gendou" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1339246-gendou"><img alt="Gendou" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1229408059p2/1339246.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/485040454?book_show_action=true">Dec 25, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Gendou" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Gendou" href="/user/show/1339246-gendou">Gendou</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781847679383" href="/book/show/9536930-incognito">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1339246-gendou?shelf=biology">biology</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1339246-gendou?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer485040454" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1089349689416101352">This book starts off with a really poor introduction. Poor, because it tries too hard, is hyperbolic, and contains two glaring errors!<br/><br/>More on those later. The book runs the gambit of freshman-level psychology with the Freud, the subconscious, chicken sexing (not as dirty as it sounds), priming, synesthesia, etc. It introduces a theory of mind based on a <b>team of rivals</b>, which is pretty neat.<br/><br/>The author puts in his two cents on the justice system. He calls for less emphasis on <b>modifiability</b> rather </span>
  <span id="freeText1089349689416101352" style="display:none">This book starts off with a really poor introduction. Poor, because it tries too hard, is hyperbolic, and contains two glaring errors!<br /><br />More on those later. The book runs the gambit of freshman-level psychology with the Freud, the subconscious, chicken sexing (not as dirty as it sounds), priming, synesthesia, etc. It introduces a theory of mind based on a <b>team of rivals</b>, which is pretty neat.<br /><br />The author puts in his two cents on the justice system. He calls for less emphasis on <b>modifiability</b> rather than <b>culpability</b> in a well-made argument. Arguably, though, he leaves out due consideration for the effect of <b>deterrence</b>.<br /><br />Inexplicably, a lot of time is spent on cosmology in the first part of the book. Eagleman introduces Leibniz as the father of calculus and the idea of a subconscious mind. Both of these are a little misleading. He also says the visible universe is "15 billion light years across". In doing so, he makes three, separate mistakes.<br /><br />First, the visible universe is 13.7 billion years old, and the estimate of 15 billion years is very out-dated for a book published in this decade.<br /><br />Second, he makes a mistake of geometry. The observable universe is a sphere, with us at the center. A sphere is twice the distance "across" as it's radius, so he should have said "30 billion light years across".<br /><br />Finally, the universe is expanding. This means distant objects are actually much farther away than their light-travel-time would suggest. Taking expansion into account, the observable universe is more like <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Observable_universe" rel="nofollow noopener">93 billion light years</a> across.<br /><br /><br /><b>Error 1: Three pounds of the most complex material we have discovered in the universe.</b><br /><br />It's hard to argue against statements like this, because it isn't clear what definition of "complex" is being used.<br /><br />First, let's assume Eagleman means to use information as the measure of complexity. There is more quantum information per unit volume in a black hole than any other object, including the human brain.<br /><br />Maybe he's talking about digital storage? If you say each synapse stores one bit, the human brain has a digital information density of <a href="http://www.ugcs.caltech.edu/~tanim/classes/ASTR342/homeworks/hw5ans.pdf" rel="nofollow noopener">10^13 bits per cubic centimeter</a>. That's about twice as dense as the theoretical upper limit of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Holographic_storage" rel="nofollow noopener">4 gigabits per cubic millimeter</a> for digital, holographic storage. Maybe this is where he gets the claim, but he's still wrong, because synapses do not store digital information! Neurological processes are messy and unreliable for digital computing, obviously.<br /><br />Maybe he's talking about branching complexity? The network of neutrons in the human brain contains hundreds of trillions of linkages, after all. What could be so huge as that, without falling apart?<br /><br />Hyphae are the microscopic, hairlike roots of a fungus. The interconnected hyphae of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Armillaria_solidipes" rel="nofollow noopener">Armillaria solidipes</a> cover 3.4 square miles of forest! This single fungal organism is thousands of years old, and I suspect a network diagram of its hyphae would be far more complex than a network diagram of a human brain.<br /><br />Also, consider the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Huge-LQG" rel="nofollow noopener">Huge-LQG</a>, the largest known structure in the universe. It has a mass of 6 * 10^18 solar masses! The average mass of a star in our galaxy is:<blockquote>m = (mass of milky way) / (number of stars)<br />m = (1,250 billion) / (300 billion)<br />m = 4.17 solar masses per star</blockquote>So, the number of stars in Huge-LQG is of the order 10^18. Each one of those stars influences its neighbors by gravitation and radiation. Therefore, I claim that this system has a "complexity" on an order (much) greater than 10^18. That's a thousand times more "complex" than the human brain, which has on the order of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neuron" rel="nofollow noopener">10^15</a> connections.<br /><br />No matter how you define complexity, Eagleman's claim is wrong.<br /><br /><br /><b>Error 2: If you represented these trillions and trillions of pulses in your brain, by a single photon of light, the combined output would be blinding.</b><br /><br />Really? Let's do the math. First, Eagleman estimates there to be <a href="http://hypertextbook.com/facts/2002/AniciaNdabahaliye2.shtml" rel="nofollow noopener">100 billion</a> neurons in the human brain. He also states that there are on the order of 100 firings per second. So, we can calculate the number of photons per second as:<br /><blockquote>N = (100 billion) * (100 per second) = 10^13 s^-1</blockquote>Let's be generous and assume this <a href="http://web.utk.edu/~cnattras/Phys250Fall2012/modules/module%201/photons.htm" rel="nofollow noopener">light</a> comes from a <a href="http://www.globalspec.com/FeaturedProducts/Detail/x/217835/0" rel="nofollow noopener">red laser</a>:<br /><blockquote>E = h * c / v<br />E = (6.626 * 10^-34) * (3 * 10^8) / (635 * 10^-9)<br />E = 3.13 * 10^-19 J<br /></blockquote>The power output of the brain-equivalent laser is:<br /><blockquote>E * N = 3.13 * 10^-6 watts</blockquote>That's one thousandth of a milliwatt! Only 0.1% the brightness of our red laser pointer. Far form being "blinding", the human eye can hardly see it.<br /><br />You see, Plank's constant is very small, even more small than the human brain is big. Eagleman is trying to use big numbers to fascinate his readers. Unfortunately for him, physics has neuroscience beet on that front.<br /><br />I don't expect Eagleman to know about things like holographic storage, hyphae, superclusters, or Planck's constant. I do expect him to stick to his subject, and not make grandiose claims outside his area of expertise, in the name of selling books. That's called intellectual fraud.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1089349689416101352" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_854491800" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/854491800" />
    <a title="Martha Love" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/14767901-martha-love"><img alt="Martha Love" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442613868p2/14767901.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/854491800?book_show_action=true">Feb 14, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Martha Love" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Martha Love" href="/user/show/14767901-martha-love">Martha Love</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer854491800" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer8425065015467800941">Eagleman raises more questions about the human condition than answers and I find this delightful. I am giving this book a 5 star rating because I think he did a superior job of citing and giving his opinions of the research in neuroscience at the time of the writing of it and because he intrigues my own mind to explore his ideas further. <br/><br/>I particularly like what Eagleman has to say about the enteric nervous system and it's importance as an example of running as a human system that is not regulat</span>
  <span id="freeText8425065015467800941" style="display:none">Eagleman raises more questions about the human condition than answers and I find this delightful. I am giving this book a 5 star rating because I think he did a superior job of citing and giving his opinions of the research in neuroscience at the time of the writing of it and because he intrigues my own mind to explore his ideas further. <br /><br />I particularly like what Eagleman has to say about the enteric nervous system and it's importance as an example of running as a human system that is not regulated by our mental ideas, nor is even accessed completely through our thinking brain. He appropriately puts this awareness in the section on "Knowing Thyself" and points out that the conscious "you" has little control over the greater physical part of oneself. As a second brain and somatic reflection enthusiast, I find his idea that you can not really know thyself simply through thinking who you are to be quite accurate and perhaps one of the most important concepts that science needs to explore further to pave the road toward healthy living.<br /><br />Martha Love<br />arthor of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13239152.What_s_Behind_Your_Belly_Button__A_Psychological_Perspective_of_the_Intelligence_of_Human_Nature_and_Gut_Instinct" title="What's Behind Your Belly Button?           <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2825213-kathryn?shelf=reading-list-2015">reading-list-2015</a>,

              <span id="reviewTextContainer609247017" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8017136007876859482">This was very enlightening - and I don’t think I’ll be able to think the same way about driving, or making choices, or anything I do or think again! I’ve mentioned this book in several conversations I’ve had with people recently, but now that I’m sitting down to write a review, I’m not sure that I can actually put my finger on exactly what I liked about this book - there was so much to take in, that a brief review can hardly do it justice.<br/><br/>Some of the things I thought were especially interesting </span>
  <span id="freeText8017136007876859482" style="display:none">This was very enlightening - and I don’t think I’ll be able to think the same way about driving, or making choices, or anything I do or think again! I’ve mentioned this book in several conversations I’ve had with people recently, but now that I’m sitting down to write a review, I’m not sure that I can actually put my finger on exactly what I liked about this book - there was so much to take in, that a brief review can hardly do it justice.<br /><br />Some of the things I thought were especially interesting were points the author made about free will - and that to a certain extent, none of us are free to do anything - we are held captive by our neural chemistry and neural connections and we do what they make us do - that is a very simplistic view - he explains it much better than I can!<br /><br />Also interesting was his point that we have kind of warring forces inside of our brains - which is why we can argue with ourselves, justify actions, get angry with ourselves etc. Which I knew anyway, but as he points out, it is this ability that makes us who we are - whether our conservative side tends to win out in these discussions or our liberal side.<br /><br />Lots of food for thought here - sparks to ignite our neural circuitry! I think I’ll have to read more books about this subject. 3.5 stars. </span>

<div id="review_405843845" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/405843845" />
    <a title="Loy Machedo" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7118702-loy-machedo"><img alt="Loy Machedo" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1470479810p2/7118702.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/405843845?book_show_action=true">Sep 02, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Loy Machedo" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Loy Machedo" href="/user/show/7118702-loy-machedo">Loy Machedo</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781847679383" href="/book/show/9536930-incognito">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer405843845" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12825076658336708564">Loy Machedo’s Book Review – Incognito by David Eagleman<br/><br/>1.	Why does your foot hit the break pedal before you are conscious of danger ahead?<br/>2.	Why do you hear your name in a conversation that you didn’t think you were listening to?<br/>3.	Why is a person whose name begins with J more likely to marry another person whose name begins with J?<br/>4.	Why is it so difficult to keep a secret?<br/>5.	How is it possible to get angry at yourself: who, exactly is mad at whom?<br/>6.	Are some marriage partners more likely to ch</span>
  <span id="freeText12825076658336708564" style="display:none">Loy Machedo’s Book Review – Incognito by David Eagleman<br /><br />1.	Why does your foot hit the break pedal before you are conscious of danger ahead?<br />2.	Why do you hear your name in a conversation that you didn’t think you were listening to?<br />3.	Why is a person whose name begins with J more likely to marry another person whose name begins with J?<br />4.	Why is it so difficult to keep a secret?<br />5.	How is it possible to get angry at yourself: who, exactly is mad at whom?<br />6.	Are some marriage partners more likely to cheat? <br />7.	Why do patients on Parkinson’s medications become compulsive gamblers? <br />8.	Why did Charles Whitman, a high-IQ bank teller and former Eagle Scout, suddenly decide to shoot forty-eight people from the University of Texas Tower in Austin?”<br /><br />Find these questions thought-provoking?<br /><br />What about these statements:<br />1.	The best way to mess up your piano piece is to concentrate on your fingers … the best way to miss the golf ball is to analyze your swing.<br />2.	When a professional baseball player connects his bat with a pitch that is traveling too fast for his conscious mind to track, he is leveraging a well-honed alien subroutine.<br />3.	No one can prove the existence of free will, our current legal system — which assumes free will when assigning blame — needs an overhaul.<br />4.	A patient suffering from Alien Hand Syndrome, for instance, may experience one hand picking up a cookie (yum) only to have the other grab it and toss it away (don't forget the diet).<br />5.	We are not born equal.<br />6.	Tiny changes in the brain, even a unexpected accident or medicine intake can cause colossal changes in behavior<br />7.	During World War II, expert British "spotters" used rapid visual-pattern analysis to recognize friendly and enemy aircraft at great distances. A method that cannot be taught or explained but can be manifested by a few.<br />8.	One in a hundred otherwise normal people, may experience something called Synesthesia – where a person may ‘hear’ colors, ‘taste’ shapes, or ‘feel’ sounds.<br /><br />If yes, then the book Incognito by David Eagleman is a Must Read for you. <br /><br />First things first:<br />Eagleman is a neuroscientist at Baylor College of Medicine and directs the Laboratory for Perception and Action. He is also the author of Wednesday is Indigo Blue: Discovering the Brain of Synesthesia and Sum: Forty Takes from the Afterlives.<br /><br />Rated by The Times as ‘The Hottest Thing in Neuroscience’, Incognito examines the unconscious part of our brains - the complex neural networks that are constantly fighting one another and influencing how we act, the things we're attracted to, and the thoughts that we have. In fact the part I loved the most was how we can learn more about our perception of reality, religion, time, consciousness and crime – all by studying just our unconscious brain!<br /><br />In "Incognito," the neuroscientist and polymath David Eagleman argues that the actions of the unconscious are so powerful and persuasive that when combined with the inescapable influences of genes, undermine our traditional ideas of self-control and free will. The ideas in the book have been presented in pithy observations, simplistic language and amazing anecdotes that leaves you absolutely convinced in what the author is trying to say. The book culminates in an argument for altering social policy and the legal system to acknowledge that we are less than fully responsible for what we do. Towards the end, you will have nothing but a total admiration for what the author has left you with so much so, that the works of Sam Harris ‘Free Will’ would come into play<br /><br />Eagleman, by imagining the future so vividly, puts into relief just how challenging neuroscience is, and will be.<br /><br />Overall, a simply amazing book with a great author and fantastic teacher. I loved the book to bits.<br /><br />Final Ratings – 10 out of 10.<br /><br />Loy Machedo<br />loymachedo.com<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12825076658336708564" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_166960345" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/166960345" />
    <a title="M0rningstar" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5370327-m0rningstar"><img alt="M0rningstar" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1362272484p2/5370327.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/166960345?book_show_action=true">May 09, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="M0rningstar" class="user" itemprop="url" name="M0rningstar" href="/user/show/5370327-m0rningstar">M0rningstar</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="ebook 9780307379788" href="/book/show/10999812-incognito">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">everyone</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5370327-m0rningstar?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer166960345" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4857753319693006851">Ever land on a question in the Never-Ending Book Quiz about a book that you've read but remember very little of? Ever find that, despite drawing a blank on the multiple choice answers, you usually get it right if you just go with the first choice that pops into your head? Ever wonder why? Then this book is for you.<br/><br/>Incognito is an engaging and eye-opening romp through fundamental questions related to human consciousness, perception, and free will, as seen through the lens of neuroscientific resea</span>
  <span id="freeText4857753319693006851" style="display:none">Ever land on a question in the Never-Ending Book Quiz about a book that you've read but remember very little of? Ever find that, despite drawing a blank on the multiple choice answers, you usually get it right if you just go with the first choice that pops into your head? Ever wonder why? Then this book is for you.<br /><br />Incognito is an engaging and eye-opening romp through fundamental questions related to human consciousness, perception, and free will, as seen through the lens of neuroscientific research. The answers are often surprising and have the potential to affect all aspects of our lives, from re-framing our understanding of the passage of time, to offering evidenced-based sentencing options in our criminal justice system. <br /><br />Despite its complex subject matter, the book's language is very accessible. It is a quick, enjoyable read. In fact, Incognito took me longer than usual to get through for a book of this length, because I'm reading it together with a friend, and some of the content is so thought-provoking that we often have to stop and discuss the topic at hand, before returning to the book an hour or two later.<br /><br />The only complaints I have are not really about the book itself. I received an uncorrected proof of Incognito as part of the Goodreads First Reads giveaway. The figures in the proof copy are of poor quality and sometimes missing altogether, which is particularly frustrating in this book because they can be crucial to understanding the text (many of them contain optical illusions, for instance.) There are of course the usual typos. Neither of these problems should affect the retail version of this book. Aside from these minor quibbles, Eagleman's writing can be a tad pedantic and repetitive at times. However, these passages can be skimmed over easily once you become accustomed to his style.<br /><br />Overall, Incognito is a fun, accessible, and thought-provoking peek into the current scientific understanding of the human consciousness.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4857753319693006851" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_276782820" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/276782820" />
    <a title="keith koenigsberg" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13526-keith-koenigsberg"><img alt="keith koenigsberg" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1211387618p2/13526.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/276782820?book_show_action=true">Feb 12, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="keith koenigsberg" class="user" itemprop="url" name="keith koenigsberg" href="/user/show/13526-keith-koenigsberg">keith koenigsberg</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781847679383" href="/book/show/9536930-incognito">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13526-keith-koenigsberg?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13526-keith-koenigsberg?shelf=science">science</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer276782820" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1904254509716737800">The first downfall of this book is, it is Malcolm Gladwellian in construction. The author pulls in anecdotes and creates his own analogies from "common sense" to make his point. After a while, you get the sense that he is just using the stories and studies which suit his purposes, and leaving the rest out. Very anecdotal. A quick look online and I found a few of his scientific assertions to be half-truths at best. What a shame.<br/><br/>The second downfall is that the author isn't half the writer that Mal</span>
  <span id="freeText1904254509716737800" style="display:none">The first downfall of this book is, it is Malcolm Gladwellian in construction. The author pulls in anecdotes and creates his own analogies from "common sense" to make his point. After a while, you get the sense that he is just using the stories and studies which suit his purposes, and leaving the rest out. Very anecdotal. A quick look online and I found a few of his scientific assertions to be half-truths at best. What a shame.<br /><br />The second downfall is that the author isn't half the writer that Malcolm Gladwell is! So he fills too many pages with fluff, and writes unpersuasively. He doesn't pull you in like Gladwell (or Freud!).<br /><br />The third downfall, (and does the author even realize it?) is that he sets out telling is in the first few chapters just how much the brain lives "a life of its own", filling in information and imagining the outside world world, when, for example, our senses are inadequate to provide details, or when disorders effect the senses. Then, in perfect parallel fashion, he goes on to draw his own conclusions and fill in a lot of philosophy that doesn't necessarily flow from the inadequate research! Eagleman is like the world-fabricating brain in his own book. Oh the irony!<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1904254509716737800" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
